<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Pre-Columbian trans-oceanic contact theories</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
</div>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For the prevailing model(s) describing the geographic origins and early migrations of humans in the Americas, see <a href="/wiki/Settlement_of_the_Americas" title="Settlement of the Americas">Settlement of the Americas</a>.</div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For more details on Native American genetic heritage, see <a href="/wiki/Genetic_history_of_indigenous_peoples_of_the_Americas" title="Genetic history of indigenous peoples of the Americas">Genetic history of indigenous peoples of the Americas</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Viking_landing.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1551" data-file-width="2292" height="149" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Viking_landing.jpg/220px-Viking_landing.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Viking_landing.jpg/330px-Viking_landing.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Viking_landing.jpg/440px-Viking_landing.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Viking_landing.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Reenactment of a Viking landing in L'Anse aux Meadows</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Claims of <b>pre-Columbian trans-oceanic contact</b> relate to visits to, the discovery of or interaction with the <a href="/wiki/Americas" title="Americas">Americas</a> and/or <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples_of_the_Americas" title="Indigenous peoples of the Americas">indigenous peoples of the Americas</a> by people from <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Asia" title="Asia">Asia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Oceania" title="Oceania">Oceania</a> before <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Columbus%27s_first_voyage" title="Columbus's first voyage">Columbus's first voyage</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Caribbean" title="Caribbean">Caribbean</a> in 1492.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RileyKelley2014_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-RileyKelley2014-1">[1]</a></sup> Such contact is generally accepted in <a href="/wiki/Prehistory" title="Prehistory">prehistory</a>, but has been hotly debated in the historic period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-science20170810_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-science20170810-2">[2]</a></sup></p>
<p>Two historical cases of pre-Columbian contact are accepted amongst the scientific and scholarly mainstream. Successful explorations led to <a href="/wiki/History_of_Greenland#Norse_settlement" title="History of Greenland">Norse settlement of Greenland</a> and the <a href="/wiki/L%27Anse_aux_Meadows" title="L'Anse aux Meadows">L'Anse aux Meadows</a> settlement in <a href="/wiki/Newfoundland_and_Labrador" title="Newfoundland and Labrador">Newfoundland</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CordellLightfoot2008_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-CordellLightfoot2008-3">[3]</a></sup> some 500 years before Columbus.</p>
<p>The scientific and scholarly responses to other post-<a href="/wiki/Prehistory" title="Prehistory">prehistory</a>, pre-Columbian contact claims have varied. Some such contact claims are examined in reputable peer-reviewed sources. Other contact claims, typically based on circumstantial and ambiguous interpretations of archaeological finds, cultural comparisons, comments in historical documents, and narrative accounts, have been dismissed as <a href="/wiki/Fringe_science" title="Fringe science">fringe science</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pseudoarcheology" title="Pseudoarcheology">pseudoarcheology</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fagan2006_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fagan2006-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancient_trans-oceanic_migration">Ancient trans-oceanic migration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pre-Columbian_trans-oceanic_contact_theories&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Ancient trans-oceanic migration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Settlement_of_the_Americas" title="Settlement of the Americas">Settlement of the Americas</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pre-clovis-sites-of-the-americas.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="536" data-file-width="536" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Pre-clovis-sites-of-the-americas.svg/220px-Pre-clovis-sites-of-the-americas.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Pre-clovis-sites-of-the-americas.svg/330px-Pre-clovis-sites-of-the-americas.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Pre-clovis-sites-of-the-americas.svg/440px-Pre-clovis-sites-of-the-americas.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pre-clovis-sites-of-the-americas.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Map of the Americas showing pre Clovis sites.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>It is now generally believed that earlier, prehistoric peopling of the Americas was achieved by boat at least 14,000 years ago, replacing the once dominant theory of ice-free corridor migration associated with the <a href="/wiki/Clovis_culture" title="Clovis culture">Clovis culture</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-science20170810_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-science20170810-2">[2]</a></sup> The world's largest general scientific society, the <a href="/wiki/American_Association_for_the_Advancement_of_Science" title="American Association for the Advancement of Science">American Association for the Advancement of Science</a> (AAAS), summarized<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-science20170810_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-science20170810-2">[2]</a></sup> for <i><a href="/wiki/Science_(journal)" title="Science (journal)">Science</a></i> in August 2017:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Most archaeologists think the first Americans arrived by boat. Now, they’re beginning to prove it. [...] Whereas archaeologists once thought that the earliest arrivals wandered into the continent through a gap in the ice age glaciers covering Canada, most researchers today think the first inhabitants came by sea. In this view, maritime explorers voyaged by boat out of Beringia—the ancient land now partially submerged under the waters of the Bering Strait—about 16,000 years ago and quickly moved down the Pacific coast, reaching Chile by at least 14,500 years ago.</p>
<div class="templatequotecite">— <cite>Lizzie Wade<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-science20170810_2-3"><a href="#cite_note-science20170810-2">[2]</a></sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>Key evidence:</p>
<ul>
<li>In 1997 a site at Monte Verde in <a href="/wiki/Chile" title="Chile">Chile</a> was measured as at least 14,500 years old — 1000 years before Clovis tools appeared. The earliest date at the site is now 18,500 years ago, though it is not universally accepted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-science20170810_2-4"><a href="#cite_note-science20170810-2">[2]</a></sup></li>
<li>Several more pre-Clovis sites have also since been discovered, such as <a href="/wiki/Huaca_Prieta" title="Huaca Prieta">Huaca Prieta</a> (<a href="/wiki/Peru" title="Peru">Peru</a>. 15,000 years), <a href="/wiki/Paisley_Caves" title="Paisley Caves">Paisley Caves</a> (<a href="/wiki/Oregon" title="Oregon">Oregon</a>. 14,300 years), and <a href="/wiki/Triquet_Island" title="Triquet Island">Triquet Island</a> (<a href="/wiki/British_Columbia" title="British Columbia">British Columbia</a>. 14,000 years).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-science20170810_2-5"><a href="#cite_note-science20170810-2">[2]</a></sup></li>
<li>Genetic evidence from precontact South American skeletons now suggests expansion out of Beringia 16,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-science20170810_2-6"><a href="#cite_note-science20170810-2">[2]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Norse_trans-oceanic_contact">Norse trans-oceanic contact</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pre-Columbian_trans-oceanic_contact_theories&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Norse trans-oceanic contact">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Norse_colonization_of_the_Americas" title="Norse colonization of the Americas">Norse colonization of the Americas</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Anne_Stine_Moe_Ingstad_(1918-1997).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1865" data-file-width="1515" height="209" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Anne_Stine_Moe_Ingstad_%281918-1997%29.jpg/170px-Anne_Stine_Moe_Ingstad_%281918-1997%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Anne_Stine_Moe_Ingstad_%281918-1997%29.jpg/255px-Anne_Stine_Moe_Ingstad_%281918-1997%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Anne_Stine_Moe_Ingstad_%281918-1997%29.jpg/340px-Anne_Stine_Moe_Ingstad_%281918-1997%29.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Anne_Stine_Moe_Ingstad_(1918-1997).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Co-discoverer <a href="/wiki/Anne_Stine_Ingstad" title="Anne Stine Ingstad">Anne Stine Ingstad</a> examines a fire pit at <a href="/wiki/L%27Anse_aux_Meadows" title="L'Anse aux Meadows">L'Anse aux Meadows</a> in 1963.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Norsemen" title="Norsemen">Norse</a> journeys to Greenland and Canada are supported by historical and archaeological evidence. A Norse colony in <a href="/wiki/Greenland" title="Greenland">Greenland</a> was established in the late 10th century, and lasted until the mid 15th century, with court and parliament assemblies (<i><a href="/wiki/Thing_(assembly)" title="Thing (assembly)">þing</a></i>) taking place at <a href="/wiki/Brattahl%C3%AD%C3%B0" title="Brattahlíð">Brattahlíð</a> and a bishop at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gardar,_Greenland" title="Gardar, Greenland">Garðar</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WurmM.C3.BChlh.C3.A4usler1996_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-WurmM.C3.BChlh.C3.A4usler1996-6">[6]</a></sup> The remains of a Norse settlement at <a href="/wiki/L%27Anse_aux_Meadows" title="L'Anse aux Meadows">L'Anse aux Meadows</a> in <a href="/wiki/Newfoundland_(island)" title="Newfoundland (island)">Newfoundland</a>, Canada, were discovered in 1960 and are dated to around the year 1000 (carbon dating estimate 990–1050 CE),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CordellLightfoot2008_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-CordellLightfoot2008-3">[3]</a></sup> L'Anse aux Meadows is the only site widely accepted as evidence of pre-Columbian trans-oceanic contact. It was named a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/World_Heritage_site" title="World Heritage site">World Heritage site</a> by <a href="/wiki/UNESCO" title="UNESCO">UNESCO</a> in 1978.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Allen1997_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Allen1997-7">[7]</a></sup> It is also notable for its possible connection with the attempted colony of <a href="/wiki/Vinland" title="Vinland">Vinland</a> established by <a href="/wiki/Leif_Erikson" title="Leif Erikson">Leif Erikson</a> around the same period or, more broadly, with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Norse_colonization_of_the_Americas" title="Norse colonization of the Americas">Norse exploration of the Americas</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kristinsson2010_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kristinsson2010-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>Few sources describing contact between <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples_of_the_Americas" title="Indigenous peoples of the Americas">indigenous peoples</a> and Norse people exist. Contact between the <a href="/wiki/Thule_people" title="Thule people">Thule people</a> (ancestors of the modern <a href="/wiki/Inuit" title="Inuit">Inuit</a>) and Norse between the 12th or 13th centuries is known. The Norse Greenlanders called these incoming settlers "<a href="/wiki/Skr%C3%A6ling" title="Skræling">skrælingar</a>". Conflict between the Greenlanders and the "skrælings" is recorded in the <i><a href="/wiki/Icelandic_Annals" title="Icelandic Annals">Icelandic Annals</a></i>. The term skrælings is also used in the Vínland sagas, which relate to events during the 10th century, when describing trade and conflict with native peoples.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Seaver2014_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Seaver2014-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Claims_of_Polynesian_contact">Claims of Polynesian contact</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pre-Columbian_trans-oceanic_contact_theories&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Claims of Polynesian contact">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Claims_involving_sweet_potato">Claims involving sweet potato</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pre-Columbian_trans-oceanic_contact_theories&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Claims involving sweet potato">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Sweet_potato" title="Sweet potato">sweet potato</a>, which is native to the Americas, was widespread in <a href="/wiki/Polynesia" title="Polynesia">Polynesia</a> when Europeans first reached the Pacific. Sweet potato has been radiocarbon-dated in the <a href="/wiki/Cook_Islands" title="Cook Islands">Cook Islands</a> to 1000 CE, and current thinking is that it was brought to central Polynesia c. 700 CE and spread across <a href="/wiki/Polynesia" title="Polynesia">Polynesia</a> from there.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> It has been suggested<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> that it was brought by Polynesians who had traveled to South America and back, or that South Americans brought it to the Pacific. It is possible that the plant could successfully float across the ocean if discarded from the cargo of a boat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phylogenetic" title="Phylogenetic">Phylogenetic</a> analysis supports the hypothesis of at least two separate introductions of sweet potatoes from South America into Polynesia, including one before and one after European contact.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> (see also <a href="#Linguistics">#Linguistics</a>.)</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Claims_involving_Peruvian_mummies">Claims involving Peruvian mummies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pre-Columbian_trans-oceanic_contact_theories&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Claims involving Peruvian mummies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A team of academics headed by the <a href="/wiki/University_of_York" title="University of York">University of York</a>'s Mummy Research Group and BioArch,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> while examining a Peruvian mummy at the <a href="/wiki/Bolton_Museum" title="Bolton Museum">Bolton Museum</a>, found that it had been embalmed using a tree <a href="/wiki/Resin" title="Resin">resin</a>. Before this it was thought that Peruvian mummies were naturally preserved. The resin, found to be that of an <i><a href="/wiki/Araucaria" title="Araucaria">Araucaria</a></i> <a href="/wiki/Pinophyta" title="Pinophyta">conifer</a> related to the <a href="/wiki/Araucaria_araucana" title="Araucaria araucana">'monkey puzzle tree'</a>, was from a variety found only in <a href="/wiki/Oceania" title="Oceania">Oceania</a> and probably <a href="/wiki/New_Guinea" title="New Guinea">New Guinea</a>. "Radiocarbon dating of both the resin and body by the University of Oxford's radiocarbon laboratory confirmed they were essentially contemporary, and date to around CE 1200."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Claims_involving_California_canoes">Claims involving California canoes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pre-Columbian_trans-oceanic_contact_theories&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Claims involving California canoes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chumash_Tomol_%27Elye%27wun_paddlers,_CINMS.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2592" data-file-width="3872" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Chumash_Tomol_%27Elye%27wun_paddlers%2C_CINMS.jpg/250px-Chumash_Tomol_%27Elye%27wun_paddlers%2C_CINMS.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Chumash_Tomol_%27Elye%27wun_paddlers%2C_CINMS.jpg/375px-Chumash_Tomol_%27Elye%27wun_paddlers%2C_CINMS.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Chumash_Tomol_%27Elye%27wun_paddlers%2C_CINMS.jpg/500px-Chumash_Tomol_%27Elye%27wun_paddlers%2C_CINMS.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chumash_Tomol_%27Elye%27wun_paddlers,_CINMS.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>‘Elye’wun</i>, a reconstructed Chumash <a href="/wiki/Tomol" title="Tomol">tomol</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Researchers including Kathryn Klar and Terry Jones have proposed a theory of contact between Hawaiians and the <a href="/wiki/Chumash_people" title="Chumash people">Chumash people</a> of <a href="/wiki/Southern_California" title="Southern California">Southern California</a> between 400 and 800 CE. The sewn-plank canoes crafted by the Chumash and neighboring <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tongva_people" title="Tongva people">Tongva</a> are unique among the indigenous peoples of North America, but similar in design to larger canoes used by Polynesians for deep-sea voyages. <i><a href="/wiki/Tomol" title="Tomol">Tomolo'o</a></i>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chumash_language" title="Chumash language">Chumash</a> word for such a craft, may derive from <i>kumula'au</i>, the Hawaiian term for the logs from which shipwrights carve planks to be sewn into canoes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> The analogous Tongva term, <i>tii'at</i>, is unrelated. If it occurred, this contact left no genetic legacy in California or Hawaii. This theory has attracted limited media attention within California, but most archaeologists of the Tongva and Chumash cultures reject it on the grounds that the independent development of the sewn-plank canoe over several centuries is well-represented in the material record.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Claims_involving_chickens">Claims involving chickens</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pre-Columbian_trans-oceanic_contact_theories&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Claims involving chickens">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The existence of chicken bones dating from 1321 to 1407 in Chile and thought to be genetically linked to South Pacific Island chicken species suggested further evidence of South Pacific contact with South America. The genetic link between the South American <a href="/wiki/Mapuche" title="Mapuche">Mapuche</a> (to whom the chickens were thought to originally belong)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> chicken bones and South Pacific Island species has been rejected by a more recent genetic study which concluded that "The analysis of ancient and modern specimens reveals a unique Polynesian genetic signature" and that "a previously reported connection between pre-European South America and Polynesian chickens most likely resulted from contamination with modern DNA, and that this issue is likely to confound ancient DNA studies involving haplogroup E chicken sequences."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<p>In recent years, evidence has emerged suggesting a possibility of pre-Columbian contact between the <a href="/wiki/Mapuche" title="Mapuche">Mapuche people</a> (Araucanians) of south-central <a href="/wiki/Chile" title="Chile">Chile</a> and Polynesians. Chicken bones found at the site <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=El_Arenal,_Chile&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="El Arenal, Chile (page does not exist)">El Arenal</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Araucan%C3%ADa_(historic_region)" title="Araucanía (historic region)">Arauco Peninsula</a>, an area inhabited by Mapuche, support a pre-Columbian introduction of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Araucana_chicken" title="Araucana chicken">chicken</a> to South America.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> The bones found in Chile were radiocarbon-dated to between 1304 and 1424, before the arrival of the Spanish. Chicken DNA sequences taken were matched to those of chickens in <a href="/wiki/American_Samoa" title="American Samoa">American Samoa</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tonga" title="Tonga">Tonga</a>, and dissimilar to European chicken.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> However, a later report in the same journal looking at the same mtDNA concluded that the Chilean chicken specimen clusters with the same European/Indian subcontinental/Southeast Asian sequences, providing no support for a Polynesian introduction of chickens to South America.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Linguistics">Linguistics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pre-Columbian_trans-oceanic_contact_theories&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Linguistics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Thames_Kumara_n.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="660" data-file-width="993" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Thames_Kumara_n.jpg/250px-Thames_Kumara_n.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Thames_Kumara_n.jpg/375px-Thames_Kumara_n.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Thames_Kumara_n.jpg/500px-Thames_Kumara_n.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Thames_Kumara_n.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Sweet potatoes for sale, Thames, New Zealand. The name "kumara" has entered <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand_English" title="New Zealand English">New Zealand English</a> from <a href="/wiki/M%C4%81ori_language" title="Māori language">Māori</a>, and is in wide use.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Dutch linguists and specialists in <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_languages_of_the_Americas" title="Indigenous languages of the Americas">Amerindian languages</a> <a href="/wiki/Willem_Adelaar" title="Willem Adelaar">Willem Adelaar</a> and Pieter Muysken have suggested that two lexical items may be shared by Polynesian languages and languages of South America. One is the name of the <a href="#Claims_involving_sweet_potato">sweet potato</a>, which was domesticated in the New World. <a href="/wiki/Proto-Polynesian_language" title="Proto-Polynesian language">Proto-Polynesian</a> *<i>kumala</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-POLLEX-kumala_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-POLLEX-kumala-27">[27]</a></sup> (compare <a href="/wiki/Rapa_Nui_language" title="Rapa Nui language">Easter Island</a> <i>kumara</i>, <a href="/wiki/Hawaiian_language" title="Hawaiian language">Hawaiian</a> <i>ʻuala</i>, <a href="/wiki/M%C4%81ori_language" title="Māori language">Māori</a> <i>kumāra</i>; apparent <a href="/wiki/Cognate" title="Cognate">cognates</a> outside <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastern_Polynesian_languages" title="Eastern Polynesian languages">Eastern Polynesian</a> may be <a href="/wiki/Loanword" title="Loanword">borrowed</a> from Eastern Polynesian languages, calling Proto-Polynesian status and age into question) may be connected with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quechua_language" title="Quechua language">Quechua</a> and <a href="/wiki/Aymara_language" title="Aymara language">Aymara</a> <i>k’umar ~ k’umara</i>. A possible second is the word for 'stone axe', Easter Island <i>toki</i>, New Zealand Maori <i>toki</i> 'adze', <a href="/wiki/Mapuche_language" title="Mapuche language">Mapuche</a> <i>toki</i>, and further afield, <a href="/wiki/Yurumangu%C3%AD_language" title="Yurumanguí language">Yurumanguí</a> <i>totoki</i> 'axe'. According to Adelaar and Muysken, the similarity in the word for sweet potato "constitutes near proof of incidental contact between inhabitants of the Andean region and the South Pacific", though according to Adelaar and Muysken the word for axe is not as convincing. The authors argue that the presence of the word for sweet potato suggests sporadic contact between Polynesia and South America, but no migrations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Similarity_of_features_and_genetics">Similarity of features and genetics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pre-Columbian_trans-oceanic_contact_theories&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Similarity of features and genetics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Isla_Mocha_1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="477" data-file-width="1110" height="95" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Isla_Mocha_1.jpg/220px-Isla_Mocha_1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Isla_Mocha_1.jpg/330px-Isla_Mocha_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Isla_Mocha_1.jpg/440px-Isla_Mocha_1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Isla_Mocha_1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Mocha_Island" title="Mocha Island">Mocha Island</a> off the coast of <a href="/wiki/Arauco_Peninsula" title="Arauco Peninsula">Arauco Peninsula</a>, Chile</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In December 2007, several human skulls were found in a museum in <a href="/wiki/Concepci%C3%B3n,_Chile" title="Concepción, Chile">Concepción, Chile</a>. These skulls originated from <a href="/wiki/Mocha_Island" title="Mocha Island">Mocha Island</a>, an island just off the coast of Chile in the Pacific Ocean, formerly inhabited by the Mapuche. <a href="/wiki/Craniometry" title="Craniometry">Craniometric</a> analysis of the skulls, according to <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Lisa_Matisoo-Smith&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Lisa Matisoo-Smith (page does not exist)">Lisa Matisoo-Smith</a> of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Otago" title="University of Otago">University of Otago</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jos%C3%A9_Miguel_Ram%C3%ADrez_Aliaga" title="José Miguel Ramírez Aliaga">José Miguel Ramírez Aliaga</a> of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Universidad_de_Valpara%C3%ADso" title="Universidad de Valparaíso">Universidad de Valparaíso</a>, suggests that the skulls have "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polynesian_people" title="Polynesian people">Polynesian</a> features" – such as a pentagonal shape when viewed from behind, and rocker jaws.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup></p>
<p>From 2007 to 2009, geneticist <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Erik_Thorsby&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Erik Thorsby (page does not exist)">Erik Thorsby</a> and colleagues have published two studies in <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tissue_Antigens" title="Tissue Antigens">Tissue Antigens</a></i> that evidence an Amerindian genetic contribution to the Easter Island population, determining that it was probably introduced before European discovery of the island.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2014, geneticist Anna-Sapfo Malaspinas of The Center for GeoGenetics at the University of Copenhagen published a study in <i><a href="/wiki/Current_Biology" title="Current Biology">Current Biology</a></i> that found human genetic evidence of contact between the populations of <a href="/wiki/Easter_Island" title="Easter Island">Easter Island</a> and South America, approximately 600 years ago (i.e. 1400 CE ± 100 years).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some members of the now extinct <a href="/wiki/Aimor%C3%A9_people" title="Aimoré people">Botocudo people</a>, who lived in the interior of Brazil, were found in research published in 2013, to have been members of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MtDNA_haplogroup" title="MtDNA haplogroup">mtDNA haplogroup</a> <a href="/wiki/Haplogroup_B_(mtDNA)#Tree" title="Haplogroup B (mtDNA)">B4a1a1</a>, which is normally found only among Polynesians and other subgroups of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Austronesian_people" title="Austronesian people">Austronesians</a>. This was based on an analysis of a 14 skulls. Two belonged to B4a1a1 (while twelve belonged to subclades of mtDNA <a href="/wiki/Haplogroup_C_(mtDNA)" title="Haplogroup C (mtDNA)">Haplogroup C1</a> common among Native Americans). The research team examined various scenarios, none of which they could say for certain were correct. They dismissed a scenario of direct contact in prehistory between Polynesia and Brazil as "too unlikely to be seriously entertained." While B4a1a1 is also found among the <a href="/wiki/Malagasy_people" title="Malagasy people">Malagasy people</a> of <a href="/wiki/Madagascar" title="Madagascar">Madagascar</a> (which experienced significant Austronesian settlement in prehistory), the authors described as "fanciful" suggestions that B4a1a1 among the Botocudo resulted from the African slave trade (which included Madagascar).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
<p>It has been confirmed that "some <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples_in_Brazil" title="Indigenous peoples in Brazil">Amazonian Native Americans</a> descend partly from a ... founding population that carried ancestry more closely related to <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_Australians" title="Indigenous Australians">indigenous Australians</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_Guineans" title="New Guineans">New Guineans</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Andaman_Islanders" title="Andaman Islanders">Andaman Islanders</a> than to any present-day <a href="/wiki/Eurasia" title="Eurasia">Eurasians</a> or Native Americans", according to a genetic study published in <i><a href="/wiki/Nature_(journal)" title="Nature (journal)">Nature</a></i> in July 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> The authors, who included <a href="/wiki/David_Reich_(geneticist)" title="David Reich (geneticist)">David Reich</a>, added: "This signature is not present to the same extent, or at all, in present-day Northern and Central Americans or in a ~12,600-year-old Clovis-associated genome, suggesting a more diverse set of founding populations of the Americas than previously accepted." This appears to conflict with an article published roughly simultaneously in <i><a href="/wiki/Science_(journal)" title="Science (journal)">Science</a></i> which adopts the previous consensus perspective. "The ancestors of all Native Americans entered the Americas as a single migration wave from Siberia no earlier than ~23 <a href="/wiki/Year#SI_prefix_multipliers" title="Year">ka</a>, separate from the Inuit and diversified into "northern" and "southern" Native American branches ~13 ka. There is evidence of post-divergence gene flow between some Native Americans and groups related to East Asians/Inuit and Australo-Melanesians .<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Claims_of_East_Asian_contact">Claims of East Asian contact</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pre-Columbian_trans-oceanic_contact_theories&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Claims of East Asian contact">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span id="Claims_of_Ecuador–East_Asian_contact"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Claims_of_Ecuador.E2.80.93East_Asian_contact">Claims of Ecuador–East Asian contact</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pre-Columbian_trans-oceanic_contact_theories&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Claims of Ecuador–East Asian contact">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A 2013 genetic study suggests the possibility of contact between <a href="/wiki/Ecuador" title="Ecuador">Ecuador</a> and <a href="/wiki/East_Asia" title="East Asia">East Asia</a>. The study suggests that the contact could have been trans-oceanic or a late-stage coastal migration that did not leave genetic imprints in North America.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-decoupling_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-decoupling-36">[36]</a></sup> This contact could explain the alleged similarity between the <a href="/wiki/Pottery" title="Pottery">pottery</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Valdivia_culture" title="Valdivia culture">Valdivia culture</a> of Ecuador and the <a href="/wiki/J%C5%8Dmon_period" title="Jōmon period">Jōmon culture</a> of <a href="/wiki/Northeast_Asia" title="Northeast Asia">Northeast Asia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-decoupling_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-decoupling-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Estrada_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Estrada-37">[37]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Claims_of_Chinese_contact">Claims of Chinese contact</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pre-Columbian_trans-oceanic_contact_theories&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Claims of Chinese contact">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Olmec_mask_at_Met.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2045" data-file-width="1525" height="228" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Olmec_mask_at_Met.jpg/170px-Olmec_mask_at_Met.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Olmec_mask_at_Met.jpg/255px-Olmec_mask_at_Met.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Olmec_mask_at_Met.jpg/340px-Olmec_mask_at_Met.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Olmec_mask_at_Met.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A jade Olmec mask. Gordon Ekholm, who was an eminent archaeologist and curator at the <a href="/wiki/American_Museum_of_Natural_History" title="American Museum of Natural History">American Museum of Natural History</a>, suggested that the Olmec art style might have originated in <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">Bronze Age</a> China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Other researchers have argued that the Olmec civilization came into existence with the help of <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">Chinese</a> refugees, particularly at the end of the <a href="/wiki/Shang_dynasty" title="Shang dynasty">Shang dynasty</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> In 1975, Betty Meggers of the <a href="/wiki/Smithsonian_Institution" title="Smithsonian Institution">Smithsonian Institution</a> argued that the Olmec civilization originated due to Shang Chinese influences around 1200 BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> In a 1996 book, Mike Xu, with the aid of Chen Hanping, claimed that <a href="/wiki/Celt_(tool)" title="Celt (tool)">celts</a> from <a href="/wiki/La_Venta" title="La Venta">La Venta</a> bear Chinese characters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> These claims are unsupported by mainstream Mesoamerican researchers.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Other claims have been made for early Chinese contact with North America.</p>
<p>In 1882 artifacts identified at the time as Chinese coins were discovered in British Columbia. A contemporary account states that:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote>
<p>In the summer of 1882 a miner found on De Foe (Deorse?) creek, Cassiar district, Br. Columbia, thirty Chinese coins in the auriferous sand, twenty-five feet below the surface. They appeared to have been strung, but on taking them up the miner let them drop apart. The earth above and around them was as compact as any in the neighborhood. One of these coins I examined at the store of Chu Chong in Victoria. Neither in metal nor markings did it resemble the modern coins, but in its figures looked more like an Aztec calendar. So far as I can make out the markings, this is a Chinese chronological cycle of sixty years, invented by <a href="/wiki/Yellow_Emperor" title="Yellow Emperor">Emperor Huungti</a>, 2637 BCE, and circulated in this form to make his people remember it.</p>
</blockquote>
<p>In 1885, a vase containing similar discs was also discovered, wrapped in the roots of a tree around 300 years old.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> Grant Keddie, Curator of Archeology at the Royal BC Museum, examined a photograph of a coin from Cassiar taken in the 1940s (whereabouts now unknown) and he believes that the character style and the evidence that it was machine-ground show it to be a 19th-century copy of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ming_Dynasty" title="Ming Dynasty">Ming Dynasty</a> temple token.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup></p>
<p>A group of Chinese Buddhist missionaries led by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hui_Shen" title="Hui Shen">Hui Shen</a> before 500 CE claimed to have visited a location called <a href="/wiki/Fusang" title="Fusang">Fusang</a>. Although Chinese mapmakers placed this territory on the Asian coast, others have suggested as early as the 1800s<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> that Fusang might have been in North America, due to perceived similarities between portions of the California coast and Fusang as depicted by Asian sources.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
<p>In his book <i>1421: The Year China Discovered the World</i>, the British author <a href="/wiki/Gavin_Menzies" title="Gavin Menzies">Gavin Menzies</a> made the controversial claim that the fleet of <a href="/wiki/Zheng_He" title="Zheng He">Zheng He</a> arrived in America in 1421.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GM_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-GM-48">[48]</a></sup> Professional historians contend that Zheng He reached the eastern coast of Africa, and dismiss Menzies's hypothesis as entirely without proof.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-finlay2004_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-finlay2004-52">[52]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1973 and 1975 doughnut-shaped stones were discovered off the coast of California that resembled stone anchors used by Chinese fishermen. These (sometimes called the <i>Palos Verdes stones</i>) were initially thought to be up to 1500 years old and proof of pre-Columbian contact by Chinese sailors. Later geological investigations showed them to be a local rock known as <a href="/wiki/Monterey_Formation" title="Monterey Formation">Monterey shale</a>, and they are thought to have been used by Chinese settlers fishing off the coast in the nineteenth century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
<p>In June 2016, <a href="/wiki/Purdue_University" title="Purdue University">Purdue University</a> published the results of research on six metal and composite metal artifacts excavated from a late prehistoric archaeological context at <a href="/wiki/Cape_Espenberg" title="Cape Espenberg">Cape Espenberg</a> on the northern coast of the <a href="/wiki/Seward_Peninsula" title="Seward Peninsula">Seward Peninsula</a> in <a href="/wiki/Alaska" title="Alaska">Alaska</a>. Also part of the research team was <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Robert_J._Speakman&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Robert J. Speakman (page does not exist)">Robert J. Speakman</a>, of the Center for Applied Isotope Studies at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Georgia" title="University of Georgia">University of Georgia</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Victor_Mair" title="Victor Mair">Victor Mair</a>, of East Asian Languages and Civilizations at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Pennsylvania" title="University of Pennsylvania">University of Pennsylvania</a>. The report is the first evidence that metal from Asia reached prehistoric North America before the contact with Europeans, stating that <a href="/wiki/X-ray_fluorescence" title="X-ray fluorescence">X-ray fluorescence</a> identified two of these artifacts as smelted industrial alloys with large proportions of tin and lead. The presence of smelted alloys in a prehistoric Inuit context in northwest Alaska was demonstrated for the first time and indicates the movement of Eurasian metal across the Bering Strait into North America before sustained contact with Europeans.</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>This is not a surprise based on oral history and other archaeological finds, and it was just a matter of time before we had a good example of Eurasian metal that had been traded [...] We believe these smelted alloys were made somewhere in Eurasia and traded to Siberia and then traded across the Bering Strait to ancestral Inuits [<i><a href="/wiki/Sic" title="Sic">sic</a></i>] people, also known as Thule culture, in Alaska. Locally available metal in parts of the Arctic, such as native metal, copper and meteoritic and telluric iron were used by ancient Inuit people for tools and to sometimes indicate status. Two of the Cape Espenberg items that were found – a bead and a buckle — are heavily leaded bronze artifacts. Both are from a house at the site dating to the Late Prehistoric Period, around 1100-1300 AD, which is before sustained European contact in the late 18th century. [...] The belt buckle also is considered an industrial product and is an unprecedented find for this time. It resembles a buckle used as part of a horse harness that would have been used in north-central China during the first six centuries before the Common Era.</p>
<div class="templatequotecite">— <cite>H. Kory Cooper, Associate Professor of Anthropology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Claims_of_Japanese_contact">Claims of Japanese contact</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pre-Columbian_trans-oceanic_contact_theories&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Claims of Japanese contact">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Otokichi.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="360" data-file-width="165" height="327" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Otokichi.jpg/150px-Otokichi.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/74/Otokichi.jpg 1.5x" width="150"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Otokichi.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Otokichi" title="Otokichi">Otokichi</a>, a Japanese castaway in America in 1832.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Smithsonian archaeologist <a href="/wiki/Betty_Meggers" title="Betty Meggers">Betty Meggers</a> wrote that pottery associated with the <a href="/wiki/Valdivia_culture" title="Valdivia culture">Valdivia culture</a> of coastal Ecuador dated to 3000–1500 BCE exhibited similarities to pottery produced during the <a href="/wiki/J%C5%8Dmon_period" title="Jōmon period">Jōmon period</a> in <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a>, arguing that contact between the two cultures might explain the similarities. Chronological and other problems have led most archaeologists to dismiss this idea as implausible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> The suggestion has been made that the resemblances (which are not complete) are simply due to the limited number of designs possible when incising clay.</p>
<p>Alaskan anthropologist Nancy Yaw Davis claims that the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zuni_people" title="Zuni people">Zuni people</a> of <a href="/wiki/New_Mexico" title="New Mexico">New Mexico</a> exhibit linguistic and cultural similarities to the Japanese.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zuni_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zuni-57">[57]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Zuni_language" title="Zuni language">Zuni language</a> is a <a href="/wiki/Language_isolate" title="Language isolate">linguistic isolate</a>, and Davis contends that the culture appears to differ from that of the surrounding natives in terms of blood type, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Endemic_disease" title="Endemic disease">endemic disease</a>, and religion. Davis speculates that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buddhist" title="Buddhist">Buddhist</a> priests or restless peasants from Japan may have crossed the Pacific in the 13th century, traveled to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/American_Southwest" title="American Southwest">American Southwest</a>, and influenced Zuni society.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zuni_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-Zuni-57">[57]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the 1890s, lawyer and politician <a href="/wiki/James_Wickersham" title="James Wickersham">James Wickersham</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> argued that pre-Columbian contact between Japanese sailors and Native Americans was highly probable, given that from the early 17th century to the mid-19th century several dozen Japanese ships were carried from Asia to North America along the powerful <a href="/wiki/Kuroshio_Current" title="Kuroshio Current">Kuroshio Currents</a>. Such Japanese ships landed from the <a href="/wiki/Aleutian_Islands" title="Aleutian Islands">Aleutian Islands</a> in the north to <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a> in the south, carrying a total of 293 persons in the 23 cases where head-counts were given in historical records. In most cases, the Japanese sailors gradually made their way home on merchant vessels, but in 1833 one Japanese crew crashed near <a href="/wiki/Cape_Flattery" title="Cape Flattery">Cape Flattery</a> and was enslaved by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Makah_people" title="Makah people">Makahs</a> for a period before being rescued by members of the <a href="/wiki/Hudson%27s_Bay_Company" title="Hudson's Bay Company">Hudson's Bay Company</a>. Another Japanese ship crashed in about 1850 near the mouth of the <a href="/wiki/Columbia_River" title="Columbia River">Columbia River</a>, Wickersham writes, and the sailors were assimilated into the local Native American population. While admitting there was no definitive proof of pre-Columbian contact between Japanese and North Americans, Wickersham thought it implausible that such contacts as outlined above would have started only after Europeans arrived in North America.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Claims_of_Indian_contact">Claims of Indian contact</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pre-Columbian_trans-oceanic_contact_theories&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Claims of Indian contact">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In 1879, <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Cunningham" title="Alexander Cunningham">Alexander Cunningham</a> described the carvings on the Stupa of <a href="/wiki/Bharhut" title="Bharhut">Bharhut</a> from c. 200 BCE and described some fruit like detail as a custard-apple (<i><a href="/wiki/Annona_squamosa" title="Annona squamosa">Annona squamosa</a></i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> He wasn't aware that botanists believed this plant to be indigenous only in the Americas, but others quickly pointed out the difficulty, as it was generally believed that the custard-apple had not been brought to India before Vasco da Gama's discovery of the sea route in 1498. This suggestion was generally disregarded but a 2009 study claimed to have found carbonized remains that date to 2000 BCE and appear like seeds of custard apple.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Smith_Copan_elephant.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="478" data-file-width="431" height="189" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Smith_Copan_elephant.jpg/170px-Smith_Copan_elephant.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Smith_Copan_elephant.jpg/255px-Smith_Copan_elephant.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Smith_Copan_elephant.jpg/340px-Smith_Copan_elephant.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Smith_Copan_elephant.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Copán stela B detail highlighted by Grafton Smith</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Grafton_Elliot_Smith" title="Grafton Elliot Smith">Grafton Elliot Smith</a> claimed that certain details in the Mayan stelae at <a href="/wiki/Cop%C3%A1n" title="Copán">Copán</a> represented an Asian elephant. He wrote <i>Elephants and Ethnologists</i>, a book on the topic in 1924. Contemporary archaeologists suggested that it was based on a tapir and his suggestions have generally been dismissed by subsequent research.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Somnathpur_si0821.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1600" data-file-width="1200" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Somnathpur_si0821.jpg/170px-Somnathpur_si0821.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Somnathpur_si0821.jpg/255px-Somnathpur_si0821.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Somnathpur_si0821.jpg/340px-Somnathpur_si0821.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Somnathpur_si0821.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Chennakesava_Temple,_Somanathapura" title="Chennakesava Temple, Somanathapura">Somnathpur</a> figures at the sides hold maize-like objects in their left hands</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Some carving details from around the 12th century in Karnataka that appeared like ears of maize (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zea_mays" title="Zea mays">Zea mays</a></i>), a crop from the New World, were interpreted by Carl Johannessen in 1989 as evidence of pre-Columbian contact.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> These suggestions were dismissed by multiple Indian researchers based on several lines of evidence. The object has been claimed by some to represent a <a href="/wiki/Muktaphala" title="Muktaphala">Muktaphala</a>, an imaginary fruit bedecked with pearls.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Claims_of_Africa_and_Middle_East_contact">Claims of Africa and Middle East contact</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pre-Columbian_trans-oceanic_contact_theories&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Claims of Africa and Middle East contact">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Claims_involving_African_contact">Claims involving African contact</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pre-Columbian_trans-oceanic_contact_theories&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Claims involving African contact">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:San_Lorenzo_Monument_4.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1632" data-file-width="2024" height="177" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/San_Lorenzo_Monument_4.jpg/220px-San_Lorenzo_Monument_4.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/San_Lorenzo_Monument_4.jpg/330px-San_Lorenzo_Monument_4.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/San_Lorenzo_Monument_4.jpg/440px-San_Lorenzo_Monument_4.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:San_Lorenzo_Monument_4.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Several <a href="/wiki/Olmec_colossal_heads" title="Olmec colossal heads">Olmec colossal heads</a> have features that some diffusionists link to African contact.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Olmec_alternative_origin_speculations" title="Olmec alternative origin speculations">Olmec alternative origin speculations</a></div>
<p>Proposed claims for an <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">African</a> presence in <a href="/wiki/Mesoamerica" title="Mesoamerica">Mesoamerica</a> stem from attributes of the <a href="/wiki/Olmec" title="Olmec">Olmec</a> culture, the presence of an African plant species in the Americas, and interpretations of certain European and Arabic historical accounts.</p>
<p>The Olmec culture existed from roughly 1200 BCE to 400 BCE. The idea that the Olmecs are related to Africans was suggested by José Melgar, who discovered the first <a href="/wiki/Olmec_colossal_heads" title="Olmec colossal heads">colossal head</a> at Hueyapan (now <a href="/wiki/Tres_Zapotes" title="Tres Zapotes">Tres Zapotes</a>) in 1862.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> More recently, <a href="/wiki/Ivan_Van_Sertima" title="Ivan Van Sertima">Ivan Van Sertima</a> has argued that these statues depict settlers or explorers from Africa, but his views have been the target of severe scholarly criticism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup></p>
<p>North African sources describe what some consider to be visits to the New World by a Mali fleet in 1311.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> According to these sources, 400 ships from the <a href="/wiki/Mali_Empire" title="Mali Empire">Mali Empire</a> discovered a land across the ocean to the West after being swept off course by ocean currents. Only one ship returned, and the captain reported the discovery of a western current to Prince <a href="/wiki/Abu_Bakr_II" title="Abu Bakr II">Abubakari II</a>; the off-course Mali fleet of 400 ships is said to have conducted both trade and warfare with the peoples of the western lands. It is claimed that Abubakari II abdicated his throne and set off to explore these western lands. In 1324, the Mali king <a href="/wiki/Musa_I_of_Mali" title="Musa I of Mali">Mansa Musa</a> is said to have told the Arabic historian <a href="/wiki/Al-Omari" title="Al-Omari">Al-Umari</a> that "his predecessors had launched two expeditions from West Africa to discover the limits of the Atlantic Ocean."</p>
<p>According to the abstract of Columbus's log made by <a href="/wiki/Bartolom%C3%A9_de_las_Casas" title="Bartolomé de las Casas">Bartolomé de las Casas</a>, the purpose of Columbus’s third voyage was to test both the claims of King <a href="/wiki/John_II_of_Portugal" title="John II of Portugal">John II of Portugal</a> that "canoes had been found which set out from the coast of Guinea [West Africa] and sailed to the west with merchandise" as well as the claims of the native inhabitants of the Caribbean island of Hispaniola that "from the south and the southeast had come black people whose spears were made of a metal called <a href="/wiki/Guan%C3%ADn_(bronze)" title="Guanín (bronze)">guanín</a>...from which it was found that of 32 parts: 18 were gold, 6 were silver, and 8 copper."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Claims_of_Pre-Clovis_immigration_from_Africa">Claims of Pre-Clovis immigration from Africa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pre-Columbian_trans-oceanic_contact_theories&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Claims of Pre-Clovis immigration from Africa">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Brazilian researcher <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Niede_Guidon" title="Niede Guidon">Niede Guidon</a>, who led the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pedra_Furada_sites" title="Pedra Furada sites">Pedra Furada sites</a> excavations "... said she believed that humans … might have come not overland from Asia but by boat from Africa".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Claims_involving_Arab_contact">Claims involving Arab contact</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pre-Columbian_trans-oceanic_contact_theories&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Claims involving Arab contact">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Early Chinese accounts of Muslim expeditions state that Muslim sailors reached a region called Mulan Pi ("magnolia skin") (<a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hant" xml:lang="zh-Hant">木蘭皮</span>; <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin" xml:lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">Mùlán Pí</span></i>; <a href="/wiki/Wade%E2%80%93Giles" title="Wade–Giles">Wade–Giles</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-wadegile" xml:lang="zh-Latn-wadegile">Mu-lan-p'i</span></i>). Mulan Pi is mentioned in <i><a href="/wiki/Lingwai_Daida" title="Lingwai Daida">Lingwai Daida</a></i> (1178) by <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Zhou_Qufei&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Zhou Qufei (page does not exist)">Zhou Qufei</a> and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zhu_fan_zhi" title="Zhu fan zhi">Zhufan Zhi</a></i> (1225) by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chao_Jukua" title="Chao Jukua">Chao Jukua</a>, together referred to as the "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sung_Document" title="Sung Document">Sung Document</a>". Mulan Pi is normally identified as Spain of the <a href="/wiki/Almoravid_dynasty" title="Almoravid dynasty">Almoravid dynasty</a> (Al-Murabitun),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> though some fringe theories hold that it is instead some part of the Americas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Needham_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-Needham-72">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Li_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-Li-73">[73]</a></sup></p>
<p>One supporter of the interpretation of Mulan Pi as part of the Americas was historian <a href="/wiki/Hui-lin_Li" title="Hui-lin Li">Hui-lin Li</a> in 1961,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Needham_72-1"><a href="#cite_note-Needham-72">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Li_73-1"><a href="#cite_note-Li-73">[73]</a></sup> and while <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Needham" title="Joseph Needham">Joseph Needham</a> was also open to the possibility, he doubted that Arab ships at the time would have been able to withstand a return journey over such a long distance across the Atlantic Ocean and points out that a return journey would have been impossible without knowledge of prevailing winds and currents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to <a href="/wiki/Muslim" title="Muslim">Muslim</a> historian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abu_al-Hasan_%27Al%C4%AB_al-Mas%27%C5%ABd%C4%AB" title="Abu al-Hasan 'Alī al-Mas'ūdī">Abu al-Hasan 'Alī al-Mas'ūdī</a> (871-957), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Khashkhash_Ibn_Saeed_Ibn_Aswad" title="Khashkhash Ibn Saeed Ibn Aswad">Khashkhash Ibn Saeed Ibn Aswad</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span dir="rtl" lang="ar" xml:lang="ar">خشخاش بن سعيد بن اسود</span>‎‎) sailed over the Atlantic Ocean and discovered a previously unknown land (<span lang="ar" xml:lang="ar">أرض مجهولة</span> <i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="Arabic transliteration">Ard Majhoola</span></i>) in 889 and returned with a shipload of valuable treasures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Claims_involving_ancient_Phoenician_contact">Claims involving ancient Phoenician contact</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pre-Columbian_trans-oceanic_contact_theories&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Claims involving ancient Phoenician contact">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Theory_of_Phoenician_discovery_of_the_Americas" title="Theory of Phoenician discovery of the Americas">Theory of Phoenician discovery of the Americas</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bat_Creek_Exam_5-28-10.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1170" data-file-width="2568" height="100" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Bat_Creek_Exam_5-28-10.JPG/220px-Bat_Creek_Exam_5-28-10.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Bat_Creek_Exam_5-28-10.JPG/330px-Bat_Creek_Exam_5-28-10.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Bat_Creek_Exam_5-28-10.JPG/440px-Bat_Creek_Exam_5-28-10.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bat_Creek_Exam_5-28-10.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Bat_Creek_inscription" title="Bat Creek inscription">Bat Creek inscription</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Scholar <a href="/wiki/Cyrus_H._Gordon" title="Cyrus H. Gordon">Cyrus H. Gordon</a> believed that <a href="/wiki/Phoenicia" title="Phoenicia">Phoenicians</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Semitic_people" title="Semitic people">Semitic</a> groups had crossed the Atlantic in antiquity, ultimately arriving in both North and South America.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> This opinion was based on his own work on the <a href="/wiki/Bat_Creek_inscription" title="Bat Creek inscription">Bat Creek inscription</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> Similar ideas were also held by <a href="/wiki/John_Philip_Cohane" title="John Philip Cohane">John Philip Cohane</a>; Cohane even claimed that many <a href="/wiki/Geography" title="Geography">geographical</a> names in <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">America</a> have a Semitic origin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup></p>
<p>Using gold obtained by expansion of the African coastal trade down the west African coast, the <a href="/wiki/Phoenicia" title="Phoenicia">Phoenician</a> state of <a href="/wiki/Carthage" title="Carthage">Carthage</a> minted gold <a href="/wiki/Stater" title="Stater">staters</a> in 350 BCE bearing a pattern, in the reverse <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Exergue" title="Exergue">exergue</a> of the coins, interpreted as a map of the Mediterranean with the Americas shown to the west across the Atlantic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> Reports of the discovery of putative Carthaginian coins in North America are based on modern replicas, that may have been buried at sites from Massachusetts to Nebraska in order to confuse and mislead archaeological investigation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Claims_of_European_contact">Claims of European contact</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pre-Columbian_trans-oceanic_contact_theories&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Claims of European contact">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Solutrean_hypothesis">Solutrean hypothesis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pre-Columbian_trans-oceanic_contact_theories&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Solutrean hypothesis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Solutrean_hypothesis" title="Solutrean hypothesis">Solutrean hypothesis</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Arch1_clovispoints2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="192" data-file-width="300" height="141" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Arch1_clovispoints2.jpg/220px-Arch1_clovispoints2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f1/Arch1_clovispoints2.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Arch1_clovispoints2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Examples of Clovis and other Paleoindian point forms, markers of archaeological cultures in northeastern North America</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Solutrean_hypothesis" title="Solutrean hypothesis">Solutrean hypothesis</a> argues that Europeans may have immigrated to the New World during the <a href="/wiki/Paleolithic" title="Paleolithic">Paleolithic</a> era, circa 16,000 to 13,000 BCE. This hypothesis proposes contact partly on the basis of perceived similarities between the flint tools of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solutrean_culture" title="Solutrean culture">Solutrean culture</a> in modern-day France, Spain and Portugal (which thrived circa 20,000 to 15,000 BCE), and the <a href="/wiki/Clovis_culture" title="Clovis culture">Clovis culture</a> of North America, which developed circa 9000 BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> The Solutrean hypothesis, first proposed in the mid-1990s, has had little support amongst the scientific mainstream,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> and subsequent genetic research has been cited as further weakening support for the idea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Claims_involving_ancient_Roman_contact">Claims involving ancient Roman contact</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pre-Columbian_trans-oceanic_contact_theories&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Claims involving ancient Roman contact">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Evidence of contacts with the civilizations of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Classical_Antiquity" title="Classical Antiquity">Classical Antiquity</a>—primarily with the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a>, but sometimes also with other cultures of the age—have been based on isolated archaeological finds in American sites that originated in the Old World. The Bay of Jars in Brazil has been yielding ancient clay storage jars that resemble <a href="/wiki/Amphora#Ancient_Rome" title="Amphora">Roman amphorae</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> for over 150 years. It has been proposed that the origin of these jars is a Roman wreck, although it has been suggested that they could be 15th or 16th century Spanish olive oil jars.</p>
<p>Romeo Hristov argues that a Roman ship, or the drifting of such a shipwreck to the American shores, is a possible explanation of archaeological finds (like the Tecaxic-Calixtlahuaca bearded head) from ancient Rome in America. Hristov claims that the possibility of such an event has been made more likely by the discovery of evidences of travels from Romans to <a href="/wiki/Tenerife" title="Tenerife">Tenerife</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lanzarote" title="Lanzarote">Lanzarote</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Canary_Islands" title="Canary Islands">Canaries</a>, and of a Roman settlement (from the 1st century BCE to the 4th century CE) on <a href="/wiki/Lanzarote" title="Lanzarote">Lanzarote island</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Tecaxic-Calixtlahuaca_head">Tecaxic-Calixtlahuaca head</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pre-Columbian_trans-oceanic_contact_theories&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Tecaxic-Calixtlahuaca head">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Tecaxic-Calixtlahuaca_head" title="Tecaxic-Calixtlahuaca head">Tecaxic-Calixtlahuaca head</a></div>
<p>A small <a href="/wiki/Terracotta" title="Terracotta">terracotta</a> head sculpture, with a beard and European-like features, was found in 1933 (in the <a href="/wiki/Toluca_Valley" title="Toluca Valley">Toluca Valley</a>, 72 kilometres southwest of <a href="/wiki/Mexico_City" title="Mexico City">Mexico City</a>) in a burial offering under three intact floors of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spanish_conquest_of_Mexico" title="Spanish conquest of Mexico">pre-colonial</a> building dated to between 1476 and 1510. The artifact has been studied by Roman art authority Bernard Andreae, director emeritus of the German Institute of Archaeology in Rome, Italy, and Austrian anthropologist <a href="/wiki/Robert_von_Heine-Geldern" title="Robert von Heine-Geldern">Robert von Heine-Geldern</a>, both of whom stated that the style of the artifact was compatible with small Roman sculptures of the 2nd century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> If genuine and if not placed there after 1492 (the pottery found with it dates to between 1476 and 1510)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> the find provides evidence for at least a one-time contact between the Old and New Worlds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to <a href="/wiki/Arizona_State_University" title="Arizona State University">ASU</a>'s Michael E. Smith,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> John Paddock, a leading Mesoamerican scholar, used to tell his classes in the years before he died that the artifact was planted as a joke by Hugo Moedano, a student who originally worked on the site. Despite speaking with individuals who knew the original discoverer (García Payón), and Moedano, Smith says he has been unable to confirm or reject this claim. Though he remains skeptical, Smith concedes he cannot rule out the possibility that the head was a genuinely buried Post-classic offering at Calixtlahuaca.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="14th-_and_15th-century_Europe_contact">14th- and 15th-century Europe contact</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pre-Columbian_trans-oceanic_contact_theories&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: 14th- and 15th-century Europe contact">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Priory_of_Sion" title="Priory of Sion">Priory of Sion</a> and <a href="/wiki/Westford_Knight" title="Westford Knight">Westford Knight</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Henry_I_Sinclair,_Earl_of_Orkney" title="Henry I Sinclair, Earl of Orkney">Henry I Sinclair, Earl of Orkney</a> and feudal baron of <a href="/wiki/Roslin_Castle" title="Roslin Castle">Roslin</a> (c. 1345 – c. 1400) was a Scottish <a href="/wiki/Nobility" title="Nobility">nobleman</a>. He is best known today because of a modern legend that he took part in explorations of <a href="/wiki/Greenland" title="Greenland">Greenland</a> and North America almost 100 years before <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Columbus" title="Christopher Columbus">Christopher Columbus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> In 1784, he was identified by <a href="/wiki/Johann_Reinhold_Forster" title="Johann Reinhold Forster">Johann Reinhold Forster</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-forst_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-forst-96">[96]</a></sup> as possibly being the Prince <a href="/wiki/Zichmni" title="Zichmni">Zichmni</a> described in letters allegedly written around the year 1400 by the <a href="/wiki/Zeno_brothers" title="Zeno brothers">Zeno brothers</a> of <a href="/wiki/Venice" title="Venice">Venice</a>, in which they describe a voyage throughout the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/North_Atlantic" title="North Atlantic">North Atlantic</a> under the command of Zichmni.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ZENO.2C_NICOL.C3.92.2C_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-ZENO.2C_NICOL.C3.92.2C-97">[97]</a></sup></p>
<p>Henry was the grandfather of <a href="/wiki/William_Sinclair,_1st_Earl_of_Caithness" title="William Sinclair, 1st Earl of Caithness">William Sinclair, 1st Earl of Caithness</a>, the builder of <a href="/wiki/Rosslyn_Chapel" title="Rosslyn Chapel">Rosslyn Chapel</a> (near <a href="/wiki/Edinburgh" title="Edinburgh">Edinburgh</a>, Scotland). The authors <a href="/wiki/Robert_Lomas" title="Robert Lomas">Robert Lomas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Knight_(author)" title="Christopher Knight (author)">Christopher Knight</a> believe some carvings in the chapel to be ears of New World corn or <a href="/wiki/Maize" title="Maize">maize</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-THK_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-THK-98">[98]</a></sup> This crop was unknown in Europe at the time of the chapel's construction, and was not cultivated there until several hundred years later. Knight and Lomas view these carvings as evidence supporting the idea that Henry Sinclair travelled to the Americas well before Columbus.
