In their book they discuss meeting with the wife of the botanist Adrian Dyer, and that Dyer's wife told him that Dyer agreed that the image thought to be maize was accurate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-THK_98-1"><a href="#cite_note-THK-98">[98]</a></sup> In fact Dyer found only one identifiable plant among the botanical carvings and suggested that the "maize" and "aloe" were stylized wooden patterns, only coincidentally looking like real plants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> Specialists in medieval architecture interpret these carvings as stylised depictions of wheat, strawberries or lilies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_ship_of_dreams_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_ship_of_dreams-101">[101]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:La_historia_general_de_las_Indias.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="483" data-file-width="326" height="296" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/La_historia_general_de_las_Indias.jpg/200px-La_historia_general_de_las_Indias.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/La_historia_general_de_las_Indias.jpg/300px-La_historia_general_de_las_Indias.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/bf/La_historia_general_de_las_Indias.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:La_historia_general_de_las_Indias.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A 1547 edition of Oviedo's <i>La historia general de las Indias.</i></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Some have conjectured that Columbus was able to persuade the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Monarchs" title="Catholic Monarchs">Catholic Monarchs</a> of <a href="/wiki/Crown_of_Castile" title="Crown of Castile">Castile</a> and <a href="/wiki/Aragon" title="Aragon">Aragon</a> to support his planned voyage only because they were aware of some recent earlier voyage across the Atlantic. Some suggest that Columbus himself visited Canada or Greenland before 1492, because according to <a href="/wiki/Bartolom%C3%A9_de_las_Casas" title="Bartolomé de las Casas">Bartolomé de las Casas</a> he wrote he had sailed 100 leagues past an island he called <a href="/wiki/Thule" title="Thule">Thule</a> in 1477. Whether he actually did this and what island he visited, if any, is uncertain. Columbus is thought to have visited <a href="/wiki/Bristol" title="Bristol">Bristol</a> in 1476.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> Bristol was also the port from which <a href="/wiki/John_Cabot" title="John Cabot">John Cabot</a> sailed in 1497, crewed mostly by Bristol sailors. In a letter of late 1497 or early 1498 the English merchant John Day wrote to Columbus about Cabot's discoveries, saying that land found by Cabot was "discovered in the past by the men from Bristol who found 'Brasil' as your lordship knows".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> There may be records of expeditions from <a href="/wiki/Bristol" title="Bristol">Bristol</a> to find the "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brazil_(mythical_island)" title="Brazil (mythical island)">isle of Brazil</a>" in 1480 and 1481.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> Trade between Bristol and Iceland is well documented from the mid 15th century.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Gonzalo_Fern%C3%A1ndez_de_Oviedo_y_Vald%C3%A9s" title="Gonzalo Fernández de Oviedo y Valdés">Gonzalo Fernández de Oviedo y Valdés</a> records several such legends in his <i>General y natural historia de las Indias</i> of 1526, which includes biographical information on Columbus. He discusses the then-current story of a Spanish caravel that was swept off its course while on its way to England, and wound up in a foreign land populated by naked tribesmen. The crew gathered supplies and made its way back to Europe, but the trip took several months and the captain and most of the men died before reaching land. The ship's pilot, a man called <a href="/wiki/Alonso_S%C3%A1nchez" title="Alonso Sánchez">Alonso Sánchez</a>, and very few others finally made it to Portugal, but all were very ill. Columbus was a good friend of the pilot, and took him to be treated in his own house, and the pilot described the land they had seen and marked it on a map before dying. People in Oviedo's time knew this story in several versions, but Oviedo regarded it as myth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1925, Soren Larsen wrote a book claiming that a joint Danish-Portuguese expedition landed in Newfoundland or Labrador in 1473 and again in 1476. Larsen claimed that <a href="/wiki/Didrik_Pining" title="Didrik Pining">Didrik Pining</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hans_Pothorst" title="Hans Pothorst">Hans Pothorst</a> served as captains, while <a href="/wiki/Jo%C3%A3o_Vaz_Corte-Real" title="João Vaz Corte-Real">João Vaz Corte-Real</a> and the possibly mythical <a href="/wiki/John_Scolvus" title="John Scolvus">John Scolvus</a> served as navigators, accompanied by <a href="/wiki/%C3%81lvaro_Martins" title="Álvaro Martins">Álvaro Martins</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> Nothing beyond circumstantial evidence has been found to support Larsen's claims.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Irish_and_Welsh_legends">Irish and Welsh legends</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pre-Columbian_trans-oceanic_contact_theories&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Irish and Welsh legends">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Great_Ireland" title="Great Ireland">Great Ireland</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Saint_brendan_german_manuscript.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="443" data-file-width="310" height="314" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Saint_brendan_german_manuscript.jpg/220px-Saint_brendan_german_manuscript.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/e8/Saint_brendan_german_manuscript.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Saint_brendan_german_manuscript.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Saint Brendan and the whale. From a 15th-century manuscript.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The legend of Saint <a href="/wiki/Brendan" title="Brendan">Brendan</a>, an Irish monk, involves a fantastical journey into the Atlantic Ocean in search of Paradise in the 6th century. Since the discovery of the New World, various authors have tried to link the Brendan legend with an early discovery of America. In 1977 The voyage was successfully recreated by <a href="/wiki/Tim_Severin" title="Tim Severin">Tim Severin</a> using an ancient Irish <a href="/wiki/Currach" title="Currach">Currach</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to a British myth, <a href="/wiki/Madoc" title="Madoc">Madoc</a> was a prince from Wales who explored the Americas as early as 1170. While most scholars consider this legend to be untrue, it was used as justification for British claims to the Americas, based on the notion of a Briton arriving before other European nationalities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup></p>
<p>Biologist and controversial amateur epigrapher <a href="/wiki/Barry_Fell" title="Barry Fell">Barry Fell</a> claims that Irish <a href="/wiki/Ogham" title="Ogham">Ogham</a> writing has been found carved into stones in the Virginias.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> Linguist <a href="/wiki/David_H._Kelley" title="David H. Kelley">David H. Kelley</a> has criticized some of Fell's work but nonetheless argued that genuine Celtic Ogham inscriptions have in fact been discovered in America.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> However, others have raised serious doubts about these claims.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_claims">Other claims</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pre-Columbian_trans-oceanic_contact_theories&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Other claims">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:0_Mosaico_pavimentale_%E2%80%93_Grotte_Celloni_%E2%80%93_Pal._Massimo.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2404" data-file-width="3606" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/0_Mosaico_pavimentale_%E2%80%93_Grotte_Celloni_%E2%80%93_Pal._Massimo.JPG/220px-0_Mosaico_pavimentale_%E2%80%93_Grotte_Celloni_%E2%80%93_Pal._Massimo.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/0_Mosaico_pavimentale_%E2%80%93_Grotte_Celloni_%E2%80%93_Pal._Massimo.JPG/330px-0_Mosaico_pavimentale_%E2%80%93_Grotte_Celloni_%E2%80%93_Pal._Massimo.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/0_Mosaico_pavimentale_%E2%80%93_Grotte_Celloni_%E2%80%93_Pal._Massimo.JPG/440px-0_Mosaico_pavimentale_%E2%80%93_Grotte_Celloni_%E2%80%93_Pal._Massimo.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:0_Mosaico_pavimentale_%E2%80%93_Grotte_Celloni_%E2%80%93_Pal._Massimo.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Floor mosaic depicting a fruit which looks like a pineapple. Opus vermiculatum, Roman artwork of the end of the 1st century BC/begin of the 1st century AD.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Claims of contact have often been based on occurrences of similar motifs in art and decoration, or on depictions in one World of species or objects that are thought to be characteristic of the other World. Famous examples include a Maya statuette claimed to depict a bearded man rowing, a cross in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bas-relief" title="Bas-relief">bas-relief</a> at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Temple_of_the_Cross" title="Temple of the Cross">Temple of the Cross</a> in <a href="/wiki/Palenque" title="Palenque">Palenque</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> Nevertheless, most of these finds can be explained as the result of misinterpretation. The Palenque "cross", for instance, is almost certainly a stylized <a href="/wiki/Maize" title="Maize">maize</a> plant.</p>
<p>In 1950, an Italian botanist, Domenico Casella, suggested that a depiction of a <a href="/wiki/Pineapple" title="Pineapple">pineapple</a> was represented among wall paintings of Mediterranean fruits at <a href="/wiki/Pompeii" title="Pompeii">Pompeii</a>. According to <a href="/wiki/Wilhelmina_Feemster_Jashemski" title="Wilhelmina Feemster Jashemski">Wilhelmina Feemster Jashemski</a>, this interpretation has been challenged by other botanists, who identify it as a pine <a href="/wiki/Conifer_cone" title="Conifer cone">cone</a> from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stone_Pine" title="Stone Pine">Umbrella pine tree</a>, which is native to the Mediterranean area.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Bat_Creek_inscription" title="Bat Creek inscription">Bat Creek inscription</a> and <a href="/wiki/Los_Lunas_Decalogue_Stone" title="Los Lunas Decalogue Stone">Los Lunas Decalogue Stone</a> have led some to suggest the possibility that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jew" title="Jew">Jewish</a> seafarers may have come to America after fleeing the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a> at the time of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jewish_Revolt" title="Jewish Revolt">Jewish Revolt</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Claims_from_Mormon_archaeology">Claims from Mormon archaeology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pre-Columbian_trans-oceanic_contact_theories&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Claims from Mormon archaeology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Izapa_Stela_5.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1523" data-file-width="1395" height="186" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Izapa_Stela_5.svg/170px-Izapa_Stela_5.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Izapa_Stela_5.svg/255px-Izapa_Stela_5.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Izapa_Stela_5.svg/340px-Izapa_Stela_5.svg.png 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Izapa_Stela_5.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Izapa Stela 5</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Archaeology_and_the_Book_of_Mormon" title="Archaeology and the Book of Mormon">Archaeology and the Book of Mormon</a></div>
<p>The <i><a href="/wiki/Book_of_Mormon" title="Book of Mormon">Book of Mormon</a></i>, a text of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latter_Day_Saint_Movement" title="Latter Day Saint Movement">Mormon religion</a> that was allegedly translated by founder <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Joseph_Smith,_Jr" title="Joseph Smith, Jr">Joseph Smith, Jr</a> from ancient gold plates in the early 1800s, states that some ancient inhabitants of the New World are descendants of Semitic peoples who sailed from the Old World. Mormon apologetics groups such as the <a href="/wiki/Foundation_for_Ancient_Research_and_Mormon_Studies" title="Foundation for Ancient Research and Mormon Studies">Foundation for Ancient Research and Mormon Studies</a> attempt to study and expand on these ideas. However, archaeologists reject these claims. The <a href="/wiki/National_Geographic_Society" title="National Geographic Society">National Geographic Society</a>, in a 1998 letter to the <a href="/wiki/Institute_for_Religious_Research" title="Institute for Religious Research">Institute for Religious Research</a>, stated "Archaeologists and other scholars have long probed the hemisphere's past and the society does not know of anything found so far that has substantiated the Book of Mormon."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> Some LDS scholars share this view, for example literature professor and active Mormon <a href="/wiki/Terryl_Givens" title="Terryl Givens">Terryl Givens</a> has pointed out that there is a lack of historical accuracy in the <i>Book of Mormon</i> as it relates to modern archaeological knowledge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Givens2004_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-Givens2004-117">[117]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the 1950s, M. Wells Jakeman popularized a belief that the <a href="/wiki/Izapa_Stela_5" title="Izapa Stela 5">Izapa Stela 5</a> represented the <a href="/wiki/Tree_of_life_vision" title="Tree of life vision">tree of life vision</a> from the Book of Mormon and he claimed it as a validation of the historicity of the claims of pre-Columbian settlement in the Americas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> His interpretations of the carving and its connection to pre-Columbian contact are not accepted as the central image of the stela actually shows a <a href="/wiki/Mesoamerican_world_tree" title="Mesoamerican world tree">Mesoamerican world tree</a>, connecting the sky above and the water or underworld below. Critics have criticized Jakeman's interpretation as belying "an obvious religious agenda that ignored Izapa Stela 5's heritage".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Trans-oceanic_travel_originating_from_the_New_World">Trans-oceanic travel originating from the New World</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pre-Columbian_trans-oceanic_contact_theories&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Trans-oceanic travel originating from the New World">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Claims_of_Egyptian_coca_and_tobacco">Claims of Egyptian coca and tobacco</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pre-Columbian_trans-oceanic_contact_theories&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Claims of Egyptian coca and tobacco">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:RAMmummy.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="984" data-file-width="1414" height="153" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/RAMmummy.jpg/220px-RAMmummy.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/RAMmummy.jpg/330px-RAMmummy.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/RAMmummy.jpg/440px-RAMmummy.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:RAMmummy.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Mummy" title="Mummy">mummy</a> of <a href="/wiki/Ramesses_II" title="Ramesses II">Ramesses II</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Traces of <a href="/wiki/Coca" title="Coca">coca</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nicotine" title="Nicotine">nicotine</a> found in some Egyptian mummies have led to speculation that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Egyptians" title="Ancient Egyptians">Ancient Egyptians</a> may have traveled to the New World. The initial discovery was made by a German <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Toxicologist" title="Toxicologist">toxicologist</a>, Svetlana Balabanova, after examining the mummy of a priestess called <a href="/wiki/Henut_Taui" title="Henut Taui">Henut Taui</a>. Follow-up tests of the hair shaft, performed to rule out contamination, gave the same results.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup></p>
<p>A television show reported that examination of numerous <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sudanese" title="Sudanese">Sudanese</a> mummies undertaken by Balabanova mirrored what was found in the mummy of Henut Taui.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Curseof_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-Curseof-121">[121]</a></sup> Balabanova suggested that the tobacco may be accounted for since it may have also been known in China and Europe, as indicated by analysis run on human remains from those respective regions. Balabanova proposed that such plants native to the general area may have developed independently, but have since gone extinct.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Curseof_121-1"><a href="#cite_note-Curseof-121">[121]</a></sup> Other explanations include fraud, though curator Alfred Grimm of the Egyptian Museum in <a href="/wiki/Munich" title="Munich">Munich</a> disputes this.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Curseof_121-2"><a href="#cite_note-Curseof-121">[121]</a></sup> Skeptical of Balabanova's findings, Rosalie David, Keeper of Egyptology at the <a href="/wiki/Manchester_Museum" title="Manchester Museum">Manchester Museum</a>, had similar tests performed on samples taken from the Manchester mummy collection and reported that two of the tissue samples and one hair sample did test positive for nicotine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Curseof_121-3"><a href="#cite_note-Curseof-121">[121]</a></sup> Sources of nicotine other than tobacco and sources of cocaine in the Old World are discussed by the British biologist Duncan Edlin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup></p>
<p>Mainstream scholars remain skeptical, and they do not see this as proof of ancient contact between Africa and the Americas, especially because there may be possible Old World sources.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Buckland_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-Buckland-124">[124]</a></sup> Two attempts to replicate Balabanova's finds of cocaine failed, suggesting "that either Balabanova and her associates are misinterpreting their results or that the samples of mummies tested by them have been mysteriously exposed to cocaine."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup></p>
<p>A re-examination in the 1970s of the mummy of <a href="/wiki/Ramesses_II" title="Ramesses II">Ramesses II</a> revealed the presence of fragments of tobacco leaves in its abdomen. This became a popular topic in fringe literature and the media and was seen as proof of contact between Ancient Egypt and the New World. The investigator, <a href="/wiki/Maurice_Bucaille" title="Maurice Bucaille">Maurice Bucaille</a>, noted that when the mummy was unwrapped in 1886 the abdomen was left open and that "it was no longer possible to attach any importance to the presence inside the abdominal cavity of whatever material was found there, since the material could have come from the surrounding environment."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> Following the renewed discussion of tobacco sparked by Balabanova's research and its mention in a 2000 publication by Rosalie David, a study in the journal <a href="/wiki/Antiquity_(journal)" title="Antiquity (journal)">Antiquity</a> suggested that reports of both tobacco and cocaine in mummies "ignored their post-excavation histories" and pointed out that the mummy of Ramesses II had been moved five times between 1883 and 1975.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Buckland_124-1"><a href="#cite_note-Buckland-124">[124]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Icelander_DNA_finding">Icelander DNA finding</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pre-Columbian_trans-oceanic_contact_theories&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Icelander DNA finding">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 2010 Sigríður Sunna Ebenesersdóttir published a genetic study showing that over 350 living Icelanders carried mitochondrial DNA of a new type that is similar to the type found only in Native American and East Asian populations. Using the <a href="/wiki/DeCODE_genetics" title="DeCODE genetics">deCODE genetics</a> database, Sigríður Sunna determined that the DNA entered the Icelandic population not later than 1700, and likely several centuries earlier. However Sigríður Sunna also states that "...while a Native American origin seems most likely for [this new haplogroup], an Asian or European origin cannot be ruled out".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Norse_legends_and_sagas">Norse legends and sagas</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pre-Columbian_trans-oceanic_contact_theories&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Norse legends and sagas">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Thorfinn_Karlsefni_1918.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2449" data-file-width="1837" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Thorfinn_Karlsefni_1918.jpg/150px-Thorfinn_Karlsefni_1918.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Thorfinn_Karlsefni_1918.jpg/225px-Thorfinn_Karlsefni_1918.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Thorfinn_Karlsefni_1918.jpg/300px-Thorfinn_Karlsefni_1918.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Thorfinn_Karlsefni_1918.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Statue of Thorfinn Karlsefni.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1009, legends report that Norse explorer <a href="/wiki/Thorfinn_Karlsefni" title="Thorfinn Karlsefni">Thorfinn Karlsefni</a> abducted two children from <a href="/wiki/Markland" title="Markland">Markland</a>, an area on the North American mainland where Norse explorers visited but did not settle. The two children were then taken to Greenland, where they were baptized and taught to speak Norse.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1420, Danish geographer <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Claudius_Clavus_Swart" title="Claudius Clavus Swart">Claudius Clavus Swart</a> wrote that he personally had seen "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pygmies" title="Pygmies">pygmies</a>" from Greenland who were caught by Norsemen in a small skin boat. Their boat was hung in <a href="/wiki/Nidaros_Cathedral" title="Nidaros Cathedral">Nidaros Cathedral</a> in <a href="/wiki/Trondheim" title="Trondheim">Trondheim</a> along with another, longer boat also taken from "pygmies". Clavus Swart's description fits the Inuit and two of their types of boats, the <a href="/wiki/Kayak" title="Kayak">kayak</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Umiak" title="Umiak">umiak</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ADE163_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-ADE163-129">[129]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ANA_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-ANA-130">[130]</a></sup> Similarly, the Swedish clergyman <a href="/wiki/Olaus_Magnus" title="Olaus Magnus">Olaus Magnus</a> wrote in 1505 that he saw in <a href="/wiki/Oslo_Cathedral" title="Oslo Cathedral">Oslo Cathedral</a> two leather boats taken decades earlier. According to Olaus, the boats were captured from Greenland pirates by one of the <a class="mw-redirect mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Haakon_(disambiguation)" title="Haakon (disambiguation)">Haakons</a>, which would place the event in the 14th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ADE163_129-1"><a href="#cite_note-ADE163-129">[129]</a></sup></p>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_Columbus" title="Ferdinand Columbus">Ferdinand Columbus</a>'s biography of his father Christopher, he says that in 1477 his father saw in <a href="/wiki/Galway" title="Galway">Galway</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ireland" title="Ireland">Ireland</a> two dead bodies which had washed ashore in their boat. The bodies and boat were of exotic appearance, and have been suggested to have been <a href="/wiki/Inuit" title="Inuit">Inuit</a> who had drifted off course.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Inuit">Inuit</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pre-Columbian_trans-oceanic_contact_theories&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Inuit">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>It has been suggested that the Norse took other indigenous peoples to Europe as slaves over the following centuries, because they are known to have taken Scottish and Irish slaves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ADE163_129-2"><a href="#cite_note-ADE163-129">[129]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ANA_130-1"><a href="#cite_note-ANA-130">[130]</a></sup></p>
<p>There is also evidence of Inuit coming to Europe under their own power or as captives after 1492. A substantial body of Greenland Inuit folklore first collected in the 19th century told of journeys by boat to <a href="/wiki/Akilineq" title="Akilineq">Akilineq</a>, here depicted as a rich country across the ocean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup></p>
<p>Pre-Columbian contact between <a href="/wiki/Alaska" title="Alaska">Alaska</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kamchatka_Peninsula" title="Kamchatka Peninsula">Kamchatka</a> via the <a href="/wiki/Subarctic" title="Subarctic">subarctic</a> <a href="/wiki/Aleutian_Islands" title="Aleutian Islands">Aleutian Islands</a> would have been conceivable, but the two settlement waves on this archipelago started on the American side and its western continuation, the <a href="/wiki/Commander_Islands" title="Commander Islands">Commander Islands</a>, remained uninhabited until after <a href="/wiki/Vitus_Bering" title="Vitus Bering">Russian explorers</a> encountered the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aleut_people" title="Aleut people">Aleut people</a> in 1741. There is no genetic or linguistic evidence for earlier contact along this route.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pre-Columbian_trans-oceanic_contact_theories&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<li id="cite_note-86"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-86">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Fagundes, Nelson J.R.; Kanitz, Ricardo; Eckert, Roberta; Valls, Ana C.S.; Bogo, Mauricio R.; Salzano, Francisco M.; Smith, David Glenn; Silva, Wilson A.; Zago, Marco A.; Ribeiro-dos-Santos, Andrea K.; Santos, Sidney E.B.; Petzl-Erler, Maria Luiza; Bonatto, Sandro L. (2008). "Mitochondrial Population Genomics Supports a Single Pre-Clovis Origin with a Coastal Route for the Peopling of the Americas". American journal of human genetics 82 (3): 583–592. <a href="/wiki/Digital_object_identifier" title="Digital object identifier">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.1016%2Fj.ajhg.2007.11.013" rel="nofollow">10.1016/j.ajhg.2007.11.013</a>. <a href="/wiki/PubMed_Central" title="PubMed Central">PMC</a> <a class="external text" href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC2427228/" rel="nofollow">2427228</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PubMed_Identifier" title="PubMed Identifier">PMID</a> <a class="external text" href="//www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/18313026" rel="nofollow">18313026</a>.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-87"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-87">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Kashani, Baharak Hooshia; Ugo A. Perego, Anna Olivieri, Norman Angerhofer, Francesca Gandini1, Valeria Carossa1, Hovirag Lancioni, Ornella Semino1, Scott R. Woodward, Alessandro Achilli, Antonio Torroni (January 2012). "Mitochondrial haplogroup C4c: A rare lineage entering America through the ice-free corridor?". American Journal of Physical Anthropology (FEE) (Wiley Periodicals) 147 (1): 34–39. <a href="/wiki/Digital_object_identifier" title="Digital object identifier">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.1002%2Fajpa.21614" rel="nofollow">10.1002/ajpa.21614</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PubMed_Identifier" title="PubMed Identifier">PMID</a> <a class="external text" href="//www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/22024980" rel="nofollow">22024980</a>. "Recent analyses of mitochondrial genomes from Native Americans have brought the overall number of recognized maternal founding lineages from just four to a current count of 15. However, because of their relative low number, almost nothing is known about some of these lineages. This leaves a considerable void in understanding the events that led to the colonization of the Americas following the Last Glacial Maximum (LGM). In this study, we identified and completely sequenced 14 mitochondrial DNAs belonging to one extremely rare Native American lineage known as haplogroup C4c. Its age and geographical distribution raise the possibility that C4c marked the Paleo-Indian group(s) that entered North America from Beringia through the ice-free corridor between the Laurentide and Cordilleran ice sheets. The similarities in ages and geographical distributions for C4c and the previously analyzed X2a lineage provide support to the scenario of a dual origin for Paleo-Indians." (subscription required)</span></li>

Pp. 238–297. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-8248-2884-4" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-8248-2884-4">978-0-8248-2884-4</a>; <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-8248-2884-4" title="Special:BookSources/0-8248-2884-4">0-8248-2884-4</a></li>
